class="mergedtoprow"><th scope="row">Website</th><td>\n<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.glasgow.gov.uk/">City website</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Glasgow</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/\xc9\x91\xcb\x90/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">\xc9\x91\xcb\x90</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span><span title="/o\xca\x8a/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">o\xca\x8a</span></span>, <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/\xc3\xa6/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">\xc3\xa6</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span></span>-, <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/\xc9\x91\xcb\x90/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">\xc9\x91\xcb\x90</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span></span>-, <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/\xc3\xa6/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">\xc3\xa6</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span></span>-/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a>: <i lang="sco">Glesga</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[\xcb\x88\xc9\xa1lez\xc9\xa1\xc9\x99]</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Gaelic language">Scottish Gaelic</a>: <i lang="gd">Glaschu</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Help:IPA/Scottish Gaelic">[\xcb\x88kl\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0as\xcc\xaa\xc9\x99xu]</a></span>) is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_towns_and_cities_in_Scotland_by_population#Localities" title="List of towns and cities in Scotland by population">most populous city</a> in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of cities in the United Kingdom">third most populous city</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, as of the 2017 estimated <a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">city</a> population of 621,020. <a href="/wiki/Shires_of_Scotland" title="Shires of Scotland">Historically</a> part of <a href="/wiki/Lanarkshire" title="Lanarkshire">Lanarkshire</a>, the city now forms the Glasgow City council area, one of the 32 <a href="/wiki/Council_areas_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Council areas of Scotland">council areas of Scotland</a>; the local authority is <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_City_Council" title="Glasgow City Council">Glasgow City Council</a>. Glasgow is situated on the <a href="/wiki/River_Clyde" title="River Clyde">River Clyde</a> in the country\'s <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">West Central Lowlands</a>. Inhabitants of the city are referred to as "Glaswegians" or "Weegies". It is the fifth most visited city in the UK.\n</p><p>Glasgow grew from a small rural settlement on the River Clyde to become the largest seaport in Britain. Expanding from the medieval <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Glasgow" title="Archbishop of Glasgow">bishopric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_burgh" title="Royal burgh">royal burgh</a>, and the later establishment of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Glasgow" title="University of Glasgow">University of Glasgow</a> in the fifteenth century, it became a major centre of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment" title="Scottish Enlightenment">Scottish Enlightenment</a> in the eighteenth century. From the eighteenth century onwards, the city also grew as one of Great Britain\'s main hubs of transatlantic trade with <a href="/wiki/British_America" title="British America">North America</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indies" title="British West Indies">West Indies</a>.\n</p><p>With the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, the population and economy of Glasgow and the surrounding region expanded rapidly to become one of the world\'s pre-eminent centres of chemicals, textiles and engineering; most notably in the <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naval_architecture" title="Naval architecture">marine engineering</a> industry, which produced many innovative and famous vessels. Glasgow was the "Second City of the British Empire" for much of the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian period</a>, although <a href="/wiki/Second_city_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Second city of the United Kingdom">many cities argue the title was theirs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the late-19th and early-20th centuries, Glasgow\'s population grew rapidly, reaching a peak of 1,127,825 people in 1938.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Urban_renewal" title="Urban renewal">urban renewal</a> projects in the 1960s, resulting in large-scale relocation of people to designated <a href="/wiki/New_town" class="mw-redirect" title="New town">new towns</a>; such as <a href="/wiki/Cumbernauld" title="Cumbernauld">Cumbernauld</a>, <a href="/wiki/Livingston,_West_Lothian" title="Livingston, West Lothian">Livingston</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Kilbride" title="East Kilbride">East Kilbride</a> and peripheral suburbs, followed by successive boundary changes, reduced the population of the City of Glasgow council area to an estimated 615,070, with 1,209,143 people living in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Glasgow" title="Greater Glasgow">Greater Glasgow</a> urban area.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The wider <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_City_Region" title="Glasgow City Region">metropolitan area</a> is home to over 1,800,000 people, equating to around 33% of Scotland\'s population. The city has one of the highest densities of any <a href="/wiki/List_of_towns_and_cities_in_Scotland_by_population#Localities" title="List of towns and cities in Scotland by population">locality</a> in Scotland at 4,023/km<sup>2</sup>.\n</p><p>Glasgow hosted the <a href="/wiki/2014_Commonwealth_Games" title="2014 Commonwealth Games">2014 Commonwealth Games</a> and is also well known in the sporting world for the <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> rivalry referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Old_Firm" title="Old Firm">Old Firm</a>, between <a href="/wiki/Celtic_F.C." title="Celtic F.C.">Celtic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rangers_F.C." title="Rangers F.C.">Rangers</a>. Glasgow is also known for the <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_patter" title="Glasgow patter">Glasgow patter</a>, a distinct dialect that is noted for being difficult to understand by those from outside the city.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#Housing"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Housing</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#Healthcare"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Healthcare</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#Education"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Education</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#Sport"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Sport</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-45"><a href="#Football"><span class="tocnumber">13.1</span> <span class="toctext">Football</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-46"><a href="#Rugby_union"><span class="tocnumber">13.2</span> <span class="toctext">Rugby union</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-47"><a href="#Rugby_league"><span class="tocnumber">13.3</span> <span class="toctext">Rugby league</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-48"><a href="#Ice_hockey"><span class="tocnumber">13.4</span> <span class="toctext">Ice hockey</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-49"><a href="#Swimming"><span class="tocnumber">13.5</span> <span class="toctext">Swimming</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-50"><a class="tocnumber">13.7</span> <span class="toctext">Other sports</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-52"><a href="#2014_Commonwealth_Games"><span class="tocnumber">13.8</span> <span class="toctext">2014 Commonwealth Games</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-53"><a href="#Major_incidents_and_tragedies"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Major incidents and tragedies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-54"><a href="#Twin_towns_and_sister_cities"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Twin towns and sister cities</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-55"><a href="#Partnerships"><span class="tocnumber">15.1</span> <span class="toctext">Partnerships</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-56"><a href="#Notable_people"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">Notable people</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-57"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-58"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-59"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">18.1</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-60"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">18.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-61"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">19</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The origin of the name Glasgow is disputed.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> It is common to derive the <a href="/wiki/Toponym" class="mw-redirect" title="Toponym">toponym</a> from the older <a href="/wiki/Cumbric_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Cumbric language">Cumbric</a> <i>glas cau</i> or a <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish" title="Middle Irish">Middle Gaelic</a> cognate, which would have meant <i>green basin</i> or <i>green valley</i>. The settlement probably had an earlier Cumbric name, <b>Cathures</b>; the modern name appears for the first time in the Gaelic period (1116), as <i>Glasgu</i>. It is also recorded that the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">King of Strathclyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhydderch_Hael" title="Rhydderch Hael">Rhydderch Hael</a>, welcomed Saint Kentigern (also known as Saint Mungo), and procured his consecration as bishop about 540. For some thirteen years Kentigern laboured in the region, building his church at the <a href="/wiki/Molendinar_Burn" title="Molendinar Burn">Molendinar Burn</a> where <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Cathedral" title="Glasgow Cathedral">Glasgow Cathedral</a> now stands, and making many converts. A large community developed around him and became known as <i>Glasgu</i> (often glossed as "the dear Green" or "dear green place").\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Glasgow" title="History of Glasgow">History of Glasgow</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_development">Origins and development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origins and development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The area around Glasgow has hosted communities for millennia, with the <a href="/wiki/River_Clyde" title="River Clyde">River Clyde</a> providing a natural location for fishing. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a> later built outposts in the area and, to keep Roman <a href="/wiki/Britannia" title="Britannia">Britannia</a> separate from the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Pictish</a> <a href="/wiki/Caledonia" title="Caledonia">Caledonia</a>, constructed the <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Wall" title="Antonine Wall">Antonine Wall</a>. Items from the wall like altars from <a href="/wiki/Castra" title="Castra">Roman forts</a> like <a href="/wiki/Balmuildy" title="Balmuildy">Balmuildy</a> can be found at the <a href="/wiki/Hunterian_Museum_and_Art_Gallery" title="Hunterian Museum and Art Gallery">Hunterian Museum</a> today.\n</p><p>Glasgow itself was reputed to have been founded by the <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionary</a> <a href="/wiki/Saint_Mungo" title="Saint Mungo">Saint Mungo</a> in the 6th century. He established a church on the <a href="/wiki/Molendinar_Burn" title="Molendinar Burn">Molendinar Burn</a>, where the present <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Cathedral" title="Glasgow Cathedral">Glasgow Cathedral</a> stands, and in the following years Glasgow became a religious centre.\nGlasgow grew over the following centuries. The <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Fair" title="Glasgow Fair">Glasgow Fair</a> reportedly began in the year 1190.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Lowest_bridging_point" title="Lowest bridging point">first bridge</a> over the River Clyde at Glasgow was recorded from around 1285, giving its name to the Briggait area of the city, forming the main North-South route over the river via <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Cross" title="Glasgow Cross">Glasgow Cross</a>. The founding of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Glasgow" title="University of Glasgow">University of Glasgow</a> in 1451 and elevation of the <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">bishopric</a> to become the <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese_of_Glasgow" title="Archdiocese of Glasgow">Archdiocese of Glasgow</a> in 1492 increased the town\'s religious and educational status and landed wealth. Its early trade was in agriculture, brewing and fishing, with cured salmon and herring being exported to Europe and the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the European Protestant <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reformation" title="Scottish Reformation">Reformation</a> and with the encouragement of the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Royal_Burghs" title="Convention of Royal Burghs">Convention of Royal Burghs</a>, the 14 incorporated trade crafts federated as the Trades House in 1605 to match the power and influence in the town council of the earlier Merchants\' Guilds who established their <a href="/w/index.php?title=Merchants_House&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Merchants House (page does not exist)">Merchants House</a> in the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Glasgow was subsequently raised to the status of <a href="/wiki/Royal_Burgh" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Burgh">Royal Burgh</a> in 1611. Glasgow\'s substantial fortunes came from international trade, manufacturing and invention, starting in the 17th century with sugar, followed by tobacco, and then cotton and linen, products of the <a href="/wiki/Triangular_trade" title="Triangular trade">Atlantic triangular slave trade</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defoe" title="Daniel Defoe">Daniel Defoe</a> visited the city in the early 18th century and famously opined in his book <i><a href="/wiki/A_tour_thro%27_the_Whole_Island_of_Great_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="A tour thro&#39; the Whole Island of Great Britain">A tour thro\' the Whole Island of Great Britain</a></i>, that Glasgow was "the cleanest and beautifullest, and best built city in Britain, London excepted". At that time the city\'s population was about 12,000, and the city was yet to undergo the massive expansionary changes to its economy and urban fabric, brought about by the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment" title="Scottish Enlightenment">Scottish Enlightenment</a> and Industrial Revolution.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trading_port">Trading port</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Trading port">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union</a> in 1707, Scotland gained further access to the vast markets of the new British Empire, and Glasgow became prominent as a hub of international trade to and from the Americas, especially in sugar, tobacco, cotton, and manufactured goods. The city\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Tobacco_Lords" title="Tobacco Lords">Tobacco Lords</a></i> created a deep water port at <a href="/wiki/Port_Glasgow" title="Port Glasgow">Port Glasgow</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Clyde" title="Firth of Clyde">Firth of Clyde</a>, as the river within the city itself was then too shallow.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> By the late 18th century more than half of the British tobacco trade was concentrated on Glasgow\'s River Clyde, with over 47,000,000&#160;lb (21,000&#160;t) of tobacco being imported each year at its peak.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> At the time, Glasgow held a commercial importance as the city participated in the trade of sugar, tobacco and later cotton.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrialisation">Industrialisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Industrialisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_(1881).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_%281881%29.jpg/250px-John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_%281881%29.jpg" width="250" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_%281881%29.jpg/375px-John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_%281881%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_%281881%29.jpg/500px-John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_%281881%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1189" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_(1881).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Shipping on the Clyde</i>, <a href="/wiki/John_Atkinson_Grimshaw" title="John Atkinson Grimshaw">Atkinson Grimshaw</a>, 1881</div></div></div>\n<p>The opening of the <a href="/wiki/Monkland_Canal" title="Monkland Canal">Monkland Canal</a> and basin linking to the <a href="/wiki/Forth_and_Clyde_Canal" title="Forth and Clyde Canal">Forth and Clyde Canal</a> at <a href="/wiki/Port_Dundas" title="Port Dundas">Port Dundas</a> in 1795, facilitated access to the extensive iron-ore and coal mines in <a href="/wiki/Lanarkshire" title="Lanarkshire">Lanarkshire</a>. After extensive <a href="/wiki/River_engineering" title="River engineering">river engineering</a> projects to dredge and deepen the River Clyde as far as Glasgow, shipbuilding became a major industry on the upper stretches of the river, pioneered by industrialists such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Napier_(engineer)" title="Robert Napier (engineer)">Robert Napier</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Elder_(shipbuilder)" title="John Elder (shipbuilder)">John Elder</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Thomson_(shipbuilder)" title="George Thomson (shipbuilder)">George Thomson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_William_Pearce,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir William Pearce, 1st Baronet">Sir William Pearce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Yarrow" title="Alfred Yarrow">Sir Alfred Yarrow</a>.\n</p><p>The River Clyde also became an important source of inspiration for artists, such as <a href="/wiki/John_Atkinson_Grimshaw" title="John Atkinson Grimshaw">John Atkinson Grimshaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Kay_(artist)" title="James Kay (artist)">James Kay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Muirhead_Bone" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Muirhead Bone">Sir Muirhead Bone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Eadie" title="Robert Eadie">Robert Eadie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Spencer" title="Stanley Spencer">Stanley Spencer</a> and <a href="/wiki/L.S._Lowry" class="mw-redirect" title="L.S. Lowry">L.S. Lowry</a>, willing to depict the new industrial era and the modern world.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glasgow_University_-Glasgow,_Scotland--LCCN2001706004.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Glasgow_University_-Glasgow%2C_Scotland--LCCN2001706004.jpg/250px-Glasgow_University_-Glasgow%2C_Scotland--LCCN2001706004.jpg" width="250" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Glasgow_University_-Glasgow%2C_Scotland--LCCN2001706004.jpg/375px-Glasgow_University_-Glasgow%2C_Scotland--LCCN2001706004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Glasgow_University_-Glasgow%2C_Scotland--LCCN2001706004.jpg/500px-Glasgow_University_-Glasgow%2C_Scotland--LCCN2001706004.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3655" data-file-height="2738" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glasgow_University_-Glasgow,_Scotland--LCCN2001706004.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>University of Glasgow in the 1890s</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glasgow_Bridge,_-Glasgow,_Scotland--LCCN2001706006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Glasgow_Bridge%2C_-Glasgow%2C_Scotland--LCCN2001706006.jpg/250px-Glasgow_Bridge%2C_-Glasgow%2C_Scotland--LCCN2001706006.jpg" width="250" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Glasgow_Bridge%2C_-Glasgow%2C_Scotland--LCCN2001706006.jpg/375px-Glasgow_Bridge%2C_-Glasgow%2C_Scotland--LCCN2001706006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Glasgow_Bridge%2C_-Glasgow%2C_Scotland--LCCN2001706006.jpg/500px-Glasgow_Bridge%2C_-Glasgow%2C_Scotland--LCCN2001706006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3674" data-file-height="2728" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glasgow_Bridge,_-Glasgow,_Scotland--LCCN2001706006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Glasgow Bridge in the 1890s</div></div></div>\n<p>Glasgow\'s population had surpassed that of Edinburgh by 1821. The development of civic institutions included the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Glasgow_Police" title="City of Glasgow Police">City of Glasgow Police</a> in 1800, one of the first municipal <a href="/wiki/Police" title="Police">police</a> forces in the world. Despite the crisis caused by the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Glasgow_Bank" title="City of Glasgow Bank">City of Glasgow Bank</a>\'s collapse in 1878, growth continued and by the end of the 19th century it was one of the cities known as the "Second City of the Empire" and was producing more than half Britain\'s tonnage of shipping<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and a quarter of all locomotives in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In addition to its pre-eminence in shipbuilding, engineering, industrial machinery, bridge building, chemicals, explosives, coal and oil industries it developed as a major centre in textiles, garment-making, carpet manufacturing, leather processing, furniture-making, pottery, food, drink and cigarette making; printing and publishing. Shipping, banking, insurance and professional services expanded at the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Glasgow became one of the first cities in Europe to reach a population of one million. The city\'s new trades and sciences attracted new residents from across the <a href="/wiki/Lowlands_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Lowlands of Scotland">Lowlands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Highlands_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Highlands of Scotland">Highlands</a> of Scotland, from <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and other parts of Britain and from <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">Continental Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During this period, the construction of many of the city\'s greatest architectural masterpieces and most ambitious civil engineering projects, such as the <a href="/wiki/Milngavie_water_treatment_works" title="Milngavie water treatment works">Milngavie water treatment works</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Subway" title="Glasgow Subway">Glasgow Subway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Corporation_Tramways" title="Glasgow Corporation Tramways">Glasgow Corporation Tramways</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_City_Chambers" title="Glasgow City Chambers">City Chambers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mitchell_Library" title="Mitchell Library">Mitchell Library</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kelvingrove_Art_Gallery_and_Museum" title="Kelvingrove Art Gallery and Museum">Kelvingrove Art Gallery and Museum</a> were being funded by its wealth. The city also held a series of <a href="/wiki/List_of_world%27s_fairs" title="List of world&#39;s fairs">International Exhibitions</a> at <a href="/wiki/Kelvingrove_Park" title="Kelvingrove Park">Kelvingrove Park</a>, in <a href="/wiki/International_Exhibition_of_Science,_Art_and_Industry" title="International Exhibition of Science, Art and Industry">1888</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_International_Exhibition_(1901)" title="Glasgow International Exhibition (1901)">1901</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Exhibition_of_National_History,_Art_and_Industry" title="Scottish Exhibition of National History, Art and Industry">1911</a>, with Britain\'s last major International Exhibition, the <a href="/wiki/Empire_Exhibition,_Scotland_1938" class="mw-redirect" title="Empire Exhibition, Scotland 1938">Empire Exhibition</a>, being subsequently held in 1938 at <a href="/wiki/Bellahouston_Park" title="Bellahouston Park">Bellahouston Park</a>, which drew 13 million visitors.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1960s_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_878125.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/1960s_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_878125.jpg/250px-1960s_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_878125.jpg" width="250" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/1960s_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_878125.jpg/375px-1960s_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_878125.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/1960s_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_878125.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="429" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1960s_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_878125.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>George Square in 1966</div></div></div>\n<p>The 20th century witnessed both decline and renewal in the city. After <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the city suffered from the impact of the <a href="/wiki/Post%E2%80%93World_War_I_recession" title="Post\xe2\x80\x93World War I recession">Post\xe2\x80\x93World War I recession</a> and from the later <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Great Depression in the United Kingdom">Great Depression</a>, this also led to a rise of radical socialism and the "<a href="/wiki/Red_Clydeside" title="Red Clydeside">Red Clydeside</a>" movement. The city had recovered by the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and grew through the post-war boom that lasted through the 1950s. By the 1960s, growth of industry in countries like Japan and <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a>, weakened the once pre-eminent position of many of the city\'s industries.\n</p><p>As a result of this, Glasgow entered a lengthy period of relative economic decline and rapid de-industrialisation, leading to high unemployment, urban decay, population decline, <a href="/wiki/Welfare_dependency" title="Welfare dependency">welfare dependency</a> and poor health for the city\'s inhabitants. There were active attempts at regeneration of the city, when the Glasgow Corporation published its controversial <i><a href="/wiki/Bruce_Report" title="Bruce Report">Bruce Report</a></i>, which set out a comprehensive series of initiatives aimed at turning round the decline of the city. The report led to a huge and radical programme of rebuilding and regeneration efforts that started in the mid-1950s and lasted into the late 1970s. This involved the mass demolition of the city\'s infamous slums and their replacement with large suburban housing estates and tower blocks.<sup id="cite_ref-Staples_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Staples-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The city invested heavily in roads infrastructure, with an extensive system of arterial roads and motorways that bisected the central area. There are also accusations that the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Office" title="Scottish Office">Scottish Office</a> had deliberately attempted to undermine Glasgow\'s economic and political influence in post-war Scotland by diverting inward investment in new industries to other regions during the <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Glen" title="Silicon Glen">Silicon Glen</a> boom and creating the <a href="/wiki/New_towns#United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="New towns">new towns</a> of Cumbernauld, Glenrothes, Irvine, Livingston and <a href="/wiki/East_Kilbride" title="East Kilbride">East Kilbride</a>, dispersed across the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">Scottish Lowlands</a> to halve the city\'s population base.<sup id="cite_ref-Staples_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Staples-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the late 1980s, there had been a significant resurgence in Glasgow\'s economic fortunes. The "<a href="/wiki/Glasgow%27s_miles_better" title="Glasgow&#39;s miles better">Glasgow\'s miles better</a>" campaign, launched in 1983, and opening of the <a href="/wiki/Burrell_Collection" title="Burrell Collection">Burrell Collection</a> in 1983 and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Exhibition_and_Conference_Centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Exhibition and Conference Centre">Scottish Exhibition and Conference Centre</a> in 1985 facilitated Glasgow\'s new role as a European centre for business services and finance and promoted an increase in tourism and inward investment.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The latter continues to be bolstered by the legacy of the city\'s <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Garden_Festival" title="Glasgow Garden Festival">Glasgow Garden Festival</a> in 1988, its status as <a href="/wiki/European_City_of_Culture" class="mw-redirect" title="European City of Culture">European City of Culture</a> in 1990, and concerted attempts to diversify the city\'s economy.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> However, it is the industrial heritage that serves as key tourism enabler.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Wider economic revival has persisted and the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Urban_renewal" title="Urban renewal">regeneration</a> of inner-city areas, including the large-scale <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Waterfront_Regeneration" title="Clyde Waterfront Regeneration">Clyde Waterfront Regeneration</a>, has led to more affluent people moving back to live in the centre of Glasgow, fuelling allegations of <a href="/wiki/Gentrification" title="Gentrification">gentrification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, the city was listed by <a href="/wiki/Lonely_Planet" title="Lonely Planet">Lonely Planet</a> as one of the world\'s top 10 tourist cities.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite Glasgow\'s economic renaissance, the <a href="/wiki/East_End_of_Glasgow" class="mw-redirect" title="East End of Glasgow">East End</a> of the city remains the focus of social deprivation.<sup id="cite_ref-breakthrough_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-breakthrough-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> A Glasgow Economic Audit report published in 2007 stated that the gap between prosperous and deprived areas of the city is widening.<sup id="cite_ref-audit_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-audit-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In 2006, 47% of Glasgow\'s population lived in the most deprived 15% of areas in Scotland,<sup id="cite_ref-audit_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-audit-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Social_Justice" title="Centre for Social Justice">Centre for Social Justice</a> reported 29.4% of the city\'s working-age residents to be "economically inactive".<sup id="cite_ref-breakthrough_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-breakthrough-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Although marginally behind the UK average, Glasgow still has a higher employment rate than Birmingham, Liverpool and Manchester.<sup id="cite_ref-audit_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-audit-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2008 the city was ranked at 43 for Personal Safety in the <a href="/wiki/Mercer_(consulting_firm)" title="Mercer (consulting firm)">Mercer</a> index of top 50 safest cities in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The Mercer report was specifically looking at Quality of Living, yet by 2011 within Glasgow, certain areas were (still) "failing to meet the Scottish Air Quality Objective levels for nitrogen dioxide (NO2) and particulate matter (PM10)".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sanitation">Sanitation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sanitation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>With the population growing, the first scheme to provide a public water supply was by the Glasgow Company in 1806. A second company was formed in 1812, and the two merged in 1838, but there was some dissatisfaction with the quality of the water supplied.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBinnie1981190_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBinnie1981190-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The Gorbals Gravitation Water Company began supplying water to residents living to the south of the River Clyde in 1846, obtained from reservoirs, which gave 75,000 people a constant water supply,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBinnie1981190_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBinnie1981190-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> but others were not so fortunate, and some 4,000 died in an outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> in 1848/1849.<sup id="cite_ref-timeline_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> This led to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Corporation_Water_Works" title="Glasgow Corporation Water Works">Glasgow Corporation Water Works</a>, with a project to raise the level of <a href="/wiki/Loch_Katrine" title="Loch Katrine">Loch Katrine</a> and to convey clean water by gravity along a 26-mile (42&#160;km) aqueduct to a holding reservoir at Milngavie, and then by pipes into the city.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBinnie1981191-192_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBinnie1981191-192-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The project cost \xc2\xa3980,000<sup id="cite_ref-timeline_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and was opened by <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> in 1859.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross-RudkinChrimes200862_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross-RudkinChrimes200862-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The engineer for the project was <a href="/wiki/John_Frederick_Bateman" title="John Frederick Bateman">John Frederick Bateman</a> while James Morris Gale became the resident engineer for the city section of the project, and subsequently became Engineer in Chief for Glasgow Water Commissioners. He oversaw several improvements during his tenure, including a second aqueduct and further raising of water levels in Loch Katrine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross-RudkinChrimes2008325_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross-RudkinChrimes2008325-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Additional supplies were provided by Loch Arklet in 1902, by impounding the water and creating a tunnel to allow water to flow into Loch Katrine. A similar scheme to create a reservoir in <a href="/wiki/Glen_Finglas" title="Glen Finglas">Glen Finglas</a> was authorised in 1903, but was deferred, and was not completed until 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-timeline_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/2002_Glasgow_floods" title="2002 Glasgow floods">2002 Glasgow floods</a>, the waterborne parasite <a href="/wiki/Cryptosporidium" title="Cryptosporidium">cryptosporidium</a> was found in the reservoir at Milngavie, and so the new <a href="/wiki/Milngavie_water_treatment_works" title="Milngavie water treatment works">Milngavie water treatment works</a> was built. It was opened by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth</a> in 2007, and won the 2007 Utility Industry Achievement Award, having been completed ahead of its time schedule and for \xc2\xa310 million below its budgeted cost.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Good health requires both clean water and effective removal of sewage. The <a href="/wiki/Caledonian_Railway" title="Caledonian Railway">Caledonian Railway</a> rebuilt many of the sewers, as part of a deal to allow them to tunnel under the city, and sewage treatment works were opened at Dalmarnoch in 1894, Dalmuir in 1904 and Sheildhall in 1910. The works experimented to find better ways to treat sewage, and a number of experimental filters were constructed, until a full activated sludge plant was built between 1962 and 1968 at a cost of \xc2\xa34 million.<sup id="cite_ref-timeline2_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline2-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Treated sludge was dumped at sea, and Glasgow Corporation owned six sludge ships between 1904 and 1998,<sup id="cite_ref-shieldhall_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shieldhall-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> when the EU <a href="/wiki/Urban_Waste_Water_Treatment_Directive" title="Urban Waste Water Treatment Directive">Urban Waste Water Treatment Directive</a> ended the practice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELobinaTerhorst200530_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELobinaTerhorst200530-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The sewerage infrastructure was improved significantly in 2017, with the completion of a tunnel 3.1 miles (5.0&#160;km) long, which provides 20&#160;million imperial gallons (90&#160;Ml) of storm water storage. It will reduce the risk of flooding and the likelihood that sewage will overflow into the Clyde during storms.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Since 2002, clean water provision and sewerage have been the responsibility of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Water" title="Scottish Water">Scottish Water</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heraldry">Heraldry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Heraldry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;background-color: #369; color: white">The coat of arms of the City of Glasgow</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Glasgow_Coat_of_Arms.png" class="image"><img alt="Glasgow Coat of Arms.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Glasgow_Coat_of_Arms.png/220px-Glasgow_Coat_of_Arms.png" width="220" height="300" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Glasgow_Coat_of_Arms.png/330px-Glasgow_Coat_of_Arms.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Glasgow_Coat_of_Arms.png/440px-Glasgow_Coat_of_Arms.png 2x" data-file-width="491" data-file-height="669" /></a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #369; color: white">Details</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Adopted</th><td>\n1866</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Crest_(heraldry)" title="Crest (heraldry)">Crest</a></th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Saint_Mungo" title="Saint Mungo">Saint Mungo</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Supporter" title="Supporter">Supporters</a></th><td>\nTwo salmon, bearing rings</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Motto" title="Motto">Motto</a></th><td>\nLet Glasgow Flourish by the preaching of Your word, and the praising of Your name.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">coat of arms</a> of the City of Glasgow was granted to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_burgh" title="Royal burgh">royal burgh</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Lyon_King_of_Arms" title="Lord Lyon King of Arms">Lord Lyon</a> on 25 October 1866.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> It incorporates a number of symbols and emblems associated with the life of Glasgow\'s patron saint, Mungo, which had been used on official seals prior to that date. The emblems represent <a href="/wiki/Saint_Mungo#Miracles" title="Saint Mungo">miracles</a> supposed to have been performed by Mungo<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and are listed in the traditional rhyme:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><i>Here\'s the bird that never flew</i></dd>\n<dd><i>Here\'s the tree that never grew</i></dd>\n<dd><i>Here\'s the bell that never rang</i></dd>\n<dd><i>Here\'s the fish that never swam</i></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>\n<p>St Mungo is also said to have preached a sermon containing the words <i>Lord, Let Glasgow flourish by the preaching of the word and the praising of thy name</i>. This was abbreviated to "Let Glasgow Flourish" and adopted as the city\'s motto.\n</p><p>In 1450, John Stewart, the first <a href="/wiki/Lord_Provost" title="Lord Provost">Lord Provost</a> of Glasgow, left an endowment so that a "St Mungo\'s Bell" could be made and tolled throughout the city so that the citizens would pray for his soul. A new bell was purchased by the magistrates in 1641 and that bell is still on display in the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Palace,_Glasgow" title="People&#39;s Palace, Glasgow">People\'s Palace</a> Museum, near <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Green" title="Glasgow Green">Glasgow Green</a>.\n</p><p>The supporters are two salmon bearing rings, and the crest is a half length figure of Saint Mungo. He wears a bishop\'s mitre and liturgical vestments and has his hand raised in "the act of <a href="/wiki/Benediction" title="Benediction">benediction</a>". The original 1866 grant placed the crest atop a helm, but this was removed in subsequent grants. The current version (1996) has a gold <a href="/wiki/Mural_crown" title="Mural crown">mural crown</a> between the shield and the crest. This form of coronet, resembling an embattled city wall, was allowed to the four area councils with city status.\n</p><p>The arms were re-matriculated by the City of <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_District_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Glasgow District Council">Glasgow District Council</a> on 6 February 1975, and by the present area council on 25 March 1996. The only change made on each occasion was in the type of coronet over the arms.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:right;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Government and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_government">Local government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Local government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_City_Council" title="Glasgow City Council">Glasgow City Council</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glasgow-George-Square.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Glasgow-George-Square.jpg/250px-Glasgow-George-Square.jpg" width="250" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Glasgow-George-Square.jpg/375px-Glasgow-George-Square.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Glasgow-George-Square.jpg/500px-Glasgow-George-Square.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3517" data-file-height="2602" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glasgow-George-Square.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Glasgow City Chambers, located on <a href="/wiki/George_Square" title="George Square">George Square</a>, is the headquarters of Glasgow City Council and the seat of local government in the city, circa 1900.</div></div></div>\n<p>Although Glasgow <a href="/wiki/Municipal_corporation" title="Municipal corporation">Corporation</a> had been a pioneer in the <a href="/wiki/Municipal_socialism" title="Municipal socialism">municipal socialist</a> movement from the late-nineteenth century, since the <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1918" title="Representation of the People Act 1918">Representation of the People Act 1918</a>, Glasgow increasingly supported <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">left-wing</a> ideas and politics at a national level. The <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Glasgow" class="mw-redirect" title="Politics of Glasgow">city council</a> was controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> for over thirty years, since the decline of the <a href="/wiki/Progressives_(Scotland)" title="Progressives (Scotland)">Progressives</a>. Since 2007, when local government elections in Scotland began to use the <a href="/wiki/Single_transferable_vote" title="Single transferable vote">single transferable vote</a> rather than the <a href="/wiki/First-past-the-post_voting" title="First-past-the-post voting">first-past-the-post system</a>, the dominance of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> within the city started to decline. As a result of the <a href="/wiki/2017_UK_local_elections" class="mw-redirect" title="2017 UK local elections">2017 UK local elections</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">SNP</a> was able to form a minority administration ending Labour\'s thirty-seven years of uninterrupted control.\n</p><p>In the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution_of_1917" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Revolution of 1917">Russian Revolution of 1917</a> and the <a href="/wiki/German_Revolution_of_1918%E2%80%9319" title="German Revolution of 1918\xe2\x80\x9319">German Revolution of 1918\xe2\x80\x9319</a>, the city\'s frequent strikes and militant organisations caused serious alarm at <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Government">Westminster</a>, with one <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_George_Square" title="Battle of George Square">uprising in January 1919</a> prompting the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>, to deploy 10,000 soldiers and tanks on the city\'s streets. A <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_George_Square" title="Battle of George Square">huge demonstration</a> in the city\'s <a href="/wiki/George_Square" title="George Square">George Square</a> on 31 January ended in violence after the <a href="/wiki/Riot_Act" title="Riot Act">Riot Act</a> was read.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Industrial_action" title="Industrial action">Industrial action</a> at the shipyards gave rise to the "<a href="/wiki/Red_Clydeside" title="Red Clydeside">Red Clydeside</a>" epithet. During the 1930s, Glasgow was the main base of the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Labour_Party" title="Independent Labour Party">Independent Labour Party</a>. Towards the end of the twentieth century, it became a centre of the struggle against the <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(Great_Britain)" title="Poll tax (Great Britain)">poll tax</a>; which was introduced in Scotland a whole year before the rest of the United Kingdom and also served as the main base of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Socialist_Party" title="Scottish Socialist Party">Scottish Socialist Party</a>, another left-wing political party in Scotland. The city has not had a <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> MP since the <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Hillhead_by-election,_1982" title="Glasgow Hillhead by-election, 1982">1982 Hillhead by-election</a>, when the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party" title="Social Democratic Party">SDP</a> took the seat, which was in Glasgow\'s most affluent area. The fortunes of the Conservative Party continued to decline into the twenty-first century, winning only one of the 79 councillors on Glasgow City Council in 2012, despite having been the controlling party (as the <a href="/wiki/Progressives_(Scotland)" title="Progressives (Scotland)">Progressives</a>) from 1969\xe2\x80\x931972 when Sir Donald Liddle was the last non-Labour <a href="/wiki/Lord_Provost_of_Glasgow" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Provost of Glasgow">Lord Provost</a>.\n</p><p>Glasgow is represented in both the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a> in Holyrood, <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>. At Westminster, it is represented by seven members of parliament (MPs), all elected at least once every five years to represent individual constituencies, using the first-past-the-post system of voting. In Holyrood, Glasgow is represented by sixteen MSPs, of whom nine are elected to represent individual constituencies once every four years using first-past-the-post, and seven are elected as additional regional members, by proportional representation. Since the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_election,_2016" title="Scottish Parliament election, 2016">Scottish Parliament election, 2016</a>, Glasgow is represented at Holyrood by\n9 <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> MSPs, 4 <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Labour_Party" title="Scottish Labour Party">Labour</a> MSPs, 2 <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Conservative_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Conservative Party">Conservative</a> MSPs and 1 <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Green_Party" title="Scottish Green Party">Scottish Green</a> MSP. In the European Parliament, the city forms part of the Scotland constituency, which elects six Members of the European Parliament.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_government">Central government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Central government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Scottish_Parliament_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Glasgow Scottish Parliament region">Glasgow Scottish Parliament region</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_constituencies" title="United Kingdom constituencies">United Kingdom constituencies</a></div>\n<p>Since Glasgow is covered and operates under two separate central governments, the devolved Scottish Parliament and UK Government, they determine various matters that Glasgow City Council is not responsible for.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scottish_Parliament">Scottish Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Scottish Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_(Scottish_Parliament_electoral_region)" title="Glasgow (Scottish Parliament electoral region)">Glasgow electoral region of the Scottish Parliament</a> covers the Glasgow City council area, the <a href="/wiki/Rutherglen" title="Rutherglen">Rutherglen</a> area of the <a href="/wiki/South_Lanarkshire" title="South Lanarkshire">South Lanarkshire</a> and a small eastern portion of <a href="/wiki/Renfrewshire" title="Renfrewshire">Renfrewshire</a>. It elects nine of the parliament\'s 73 <a href="/wiki/First_past_the_post" class="mw-redirect" title="First past the post">first past the post</a> constituency members and seven of the 56 <a href="/wiki/Mixed_member_proportional_representation" class="mw-redirect" title="Mixed member proportional representation">additional members</a>. Both kinds of member are known as <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Scottish_Parliament" title="Member of the Scottish Parliament">Members of the Scottish Parliament (MSPs)</a>. The system of election is designed to produce a form of <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a>.\n</p><p>The first past the post seats were created in 1999 with the names and boundaries of then existing <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Westminster</a> (<a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>) constituencies. In 2005, the number of Westminster <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament">Members of Parliament (MPs)</a> representing Scotland was cut to 59, with new constituencies being formed, while the existing number of <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Scottish_Parliament" title="Member of the Scottish Parliament">MSPs</a> was retained at Holyrood. In the 2011 Scottish Parliament election, the boundaries of the Glasgow region were redrawn.\n</p><p>Currently, the nine Scottish Parliament constituencies in the Glasgow electoral region are:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Anniesland_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow Anniesland (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Glasgow Anniesland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Cathcart_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow Cathcart (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Glasgow Cathcart</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Kelvin_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow Kelvin (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Glasgow Kelvin</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Maryhill_and_Springburn_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow Maryhill and Springburn (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Glasgow Maryhill and Springburn</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Pollok_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow Pollok (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Glasgow Pollok</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Provan_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow Provan (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Glasgow Provan</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Shettleston_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow Shettleston (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Glasgow Shettleston</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Southside_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow Southside (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Glasgow Southside</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Rutherglen_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Rutherglen (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Rutherglen</a></li></ul>\n<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_election,_2016" title="Scottish Parliament election, 2016">2016 Scottish Parliament election</a>, all nine of these constituencies were won by <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> MSPs. On the regional vote, the Glasgow electoral region is represented by four <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Labour" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Labour">Labour</a> MSPs, two <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Conservative_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Conservative Party">Conservative</a> MSPs and one <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Green_Party" title="Scottish Green Party">Green</a> MSP.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Westminster_Parliament">Westminster Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Westminster Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Following reform of constituencies of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland">House of Commons</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a> (<a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Westminster</a>) in 2005, which reduced the number of Scottish <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament">Members of Parliament (MPs)</a>, the current Westminster constituencies representing Glasgow are:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Central_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow Central (UK Parliament constituency)">Glasgow Central</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_East_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow East (UK Parliament constituency)">Glasgow East</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_North_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow North (UK Parliament constituency)">Glasgow North</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_North_East_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow North East (UK Parliament constituency)">Glasgow North East</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_North_West_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow North West (UK Parliament constituency)">Glasgow North West</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_South_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow South (UK Parliament constituency)">Glasgow South</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_South_West_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow South West (UK Parliament constituency)">Glasgow South West</a></li></ul>\n<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/2014_Scottish_independence_referendum" class="mw-redirect" title="2014 Scottish independence referendum">2014 Scottish independence referendum</a>, in which 53.49% of the electorate of Glasgow voted in favour of Scottish independence; the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">SNP</a> won every constituency in the city at the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015 general election</a>, including a record-breaking 39.3% swing from Labour to SNP in the seat of <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_North_East_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Glasgow North East (UK Parliament constituency)">Glasgow North East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the <a href="/wiki/2017_UK_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="2017 UK general election">2017 snap election</a>, Glasgow is currently represented by 6 Scottish National Party MPs and 1 Labour MP; the Glasgow North East constituency which had a record 39.3% swing from Labour to SNP at the previous general election, was regained by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Sweeney" title="Paul Sweeney">Paul Sweeney</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a>, who narrowly defeated sitting SNP MP <a href="/wiki/Anne_McLaughlin" title="Anne McLaughlin">Anne McLaughlin</a> by 242 votes.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Referendums">Referendums</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Referendums">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence_referendum" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish independence referendum">Scottish independence referendum</a>, Glasgow voted "Yes" by a margin of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence_referendum,_2014#By_area" title="Scottish independence referendum, 2014">53.5% to 46.5%</a>.\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">Brexit referendum</a>, results varied from constituency to constituency. Glasgow North recorded the biggest remain vote with 78% opting to stay in the EU whilst in Glasgow East this figure dropped to 56% \xe2\x80\x93 the smallest remain vote in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The city as a whole voted to remain in the EU, by 66.6% to 33.3%.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turnout">Turnout</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Turnout">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Voter_turnout" title="Voter turnout">Voter turnout</a> has often been lower in Glasgow than in the rest of the United Kingdom. In the Referendum of 2014 turnout was 75%, the lowest in Scotland;<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">Brexit referendum</a> the city\'s voters, while joining the rest of Scotland in voting to remain part of the EU, again had a low turnout of 56.2%, although SNP MP <a href="/wiki/Angus_Robertson" title="Angus Robertson">Angus Robertson</a> placed this in the historical context of traditional low turnout in Glasgow.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 2015 general election, the six Scottish constituencies with the lowest turnout were all in Glasgow;<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> turnout further decreased in the 2017 election, when five of the city\'s seven seats reported a lowered turnout.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Glasgow" title="Geography of Glasgow">Geography of Glasgow</a></div>\n<p>Glasgow is located on the banks of the River Clyde, in <a href="/wiki/Strathclyde" title="Strathclyde">West Central</a> Scotland. Its second most important river is the <a href="/wiki/River_Kelvin" title="River Kelvin">Kelvin</a> whose name was used in creating the title of <a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">Baron Kelvin</a> and thereby ended up as the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">SI unit of temperature</a>.\nOn older maps Glasgow is shown within the area of the pre-1975 county of <a href="/wiki/Lanarkshire" title="Lanarkshire">Lanarkshire</a>; from 1975 to 1996 it appears within <a href="/wiki/Strathclyde_Region" class="mw-redirect" title="Strathclyde Region">Strathclyde Region</a>; more recent maps generally show Glasgow as one of 32 <a href="/wiki/Subdivisions_of_Scotland" title="Subdivisions of Scotland">Council Areas</a> in Scotland.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:808px;">\n<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glasgow_panorama.jpg" class="image" title="Glasgow city centre panorama from The Lighthouse"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Glasgow_panorama.jpg/800px-Glasgow_panorama.jpg" width="800" height="244" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e5/Glasgow_panorama.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1152" data-file-height="351" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glasgow_panorama.jpg" title="File:Glasgow panorama.jpg"> </a></div>Glasgow city centre panorama from The Lighthouse</div>\n</div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location">Location</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" href="/wiki/Strathaven" title="Strathaven">Strathaven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cumnock" title="Cumnock">Cumnock</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Motherwell" title="Motherwell">Motherwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_South_Lanarkshire" title="Hamilton, South Lanarkshire">Hamilton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanark" title="Lanark">Lanark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wishaw" title="Wishaw">Wishaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carluke" title="Carluke">Carluke</a></td>\n</tr></tbody></table></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<p>Glasgow is located in the central belt of Scotland.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glasgow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Despite its northerly latitude, similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, Glasgow\'s climate is classified as <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">oceanic</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification">K\xc3\xb6ppen</a> <b>Cfb</b>).\nData is available online for 3 official weather stations in the Glasgow area: Paisley, Abbotsinch and Bishopton. All are located to the West of the city centre.\nOwing to its westerly position and proximity to the Atlantic Ocean, Glasgow is one of Scotland\'s milder areas. Temperatures are usually higher than in most places of equal latitude away from the UK, due to the warming influence of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>. However, this results in less distinct seasons as compared to much of Western Europe. At Paisley, the annual precipitation averages 1,245 millimetres (49.0&#160;in)\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Glasgow_from_Queens_Park.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/View_of_Glasgow_from_Queens_Park.jpg/250px-View_of_Glasgow_from_Queens_Park.jpg" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/View_of_Glasgow_from_Queens_Park.jpg/375px-View_of_Glasgow_from_Queens_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/View_of_Glasgow_from_Queens_Park.jpg/500px-View_of_Glasgow_from_Queens_Park.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Glasgow_from_Queens_Park.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Panorama over Glasgow\'s South Side and West End from <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Park,_Glasgow" title="Queen&#39;s Park, Glasgow">Queen\'s Park</a>, looking north west. Left of centre can be seen the <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Arc" title="Clyde Arc">Clyde Arc</a> bridge at Finnieston, while beyond is the tower of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Glasgow" title="University of Glasgow">University of Glasgow</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Campsie_Fells" title="Campsie Fells">Campsie Fells</a> in the distance on the right.</div></div></div>\n<p>Winters are cool and overcast, with a January mean of 5.0&#160;\xc2\xb0C (41.0&#160;\xc2\xb0F), though lows sometimes fall below freezing. Since 2000 Glasgow has experienced few very cold, snowy and harsh winters where temperatures have fallen much below freezing. The most extreme instances have however seen temperatures around \xe2\x88\x9212&#160;\xc2\xb0C (10&#160;\xc2\xb0F) in the area. Snowfall accumulation is infrequent and short-lived. The spring months (March to May) are usually mild and often quite pleasant. Many of Glasgow\'s trees and plants begin to flower at this time of the year and parks and gardens are filled with spring colours.\n</p><p>During the summer months (June to August) the weather can vary considerably from day to day, ranging from relatively cool and wet to quite warm with the odd sunny day. Long dry spells of warm weather are generally quite scarce. Overcast and humid conditions without rain are frequent. Generally the weather pattern is quite unsettled and erratic during these months, with only occasional heatwaves. The warmest month is usually July, with average highs above 20&#160;\xc2\xb0C (68&#160;\xc2\xb0F). Summer days can occasionally reach up to 27&#160;\xc2\xb0C (81&#160;\xc2\xb0F), and very rarely exceed 30&#160;\xc2\xb0C (86&#160;\xc2\xb0F). Autumns are generally cool to mild with increasing precipitation. During early autumn there can be some settled periods of weather and it can feel pleasant with mild temperatures and some sunny days.\n</p><p>The official Met Office data series goes back to 1959 and shows that there only have been a few warm and no hot summers in Glasgow, in stark contrast to areas further south in Great Britain and eastwards in Europe. The warmest month on record in the data series is July 2006, with an average high of 22.7&#160;\xc2\xb0C (72.9&#160;\xc2\xb0F) and low of 13.7&#160;\xc2\xb0C (56.7&#160;\xc2\xb0F).<sup id="cite_ref-Climate_Station_Data_for_Paisley_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Climate_Station_Data_for_Paisley-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Even this extreme event only matched a normal summer on similar parallels in continental Europe, underlining the maritime influences. The coldest month on record since the data series began is December 2010, during a severe <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_2010%E2%80%9311_in_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="Winter of 2010\xe2\x80\x9311 in Great Britain and Ireland">cold wave</a> affecting the British Isles. Even then, the December high was above freezing at 1.6&#160;\xc2\xb0C (34.9&#160;\xc2\xb0F) with the low of \xe2\x88\x924.4&#160;\xc2\xb0C (24.1&#160;\xc2\xb0F).<sup id="cite_ref-Climate_Station_Data_for_Paisley_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Climate_Station_Data_for_Paisley-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> This still ensured Glasgow\'s coldest month of 2010 remained milder than the isotherm of \xe2\x88\x923&#160;\xc2\xb0C (27&#160;\xc2\xb0F) normally used to determine continental climate normals.\n</p><p>Temperature extremes have ranged from \xe2\x88\x9219.9&#160;\xc2\xb0C (\xe2\x88\x924&#160;\xc2\xb0F) to 31.2&#160;\xc2\xb0C (88&#160;\xc2\xb0F), at Abbotsinch,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and \xe2\x88\x9214.8&#160;\xc2\xb0C (5&#160;\xc2\xb0F) to 31.0&#160;\xc2\xb0C (88&#160;\xc2\xb0F) at Paisley.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The coldest temperature to have occurred in recent years was \xe2\x88\x9212.5&#160;\xc2\xb0C (9.5&#160;\xc2\xb0F) at Bishopton during December 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\nHowever, the highest temperature ever recorded was 31.9&#160;\xc2\xb0C (89&#160;\xc2\xb0F) at Bishopton on 28 June 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<p><br />\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible" style="width:90%;
